        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3506381147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5656736493685801647">Hmm. I read Iron Weed about 30 years ago, not realizing it was part of a trilogy. This one, the Story of Jack Legs Diamond is part 1 of the trilogy. When I saw it on the shelf, I actually thought it was the one I'd read before. Not once I opened it. The era is the same, but the characters couldn't be more differnt. Any desire to read #2? No, not really, even if it was sitting on my shelf, I would not go directly there. Legs was an actual gangster who lived in died in the author's home town of Al</span>
  <span id="freeText5656736493685801647" style="display:none">Hmm. I read Iron Weed about 30 years ago, not realizing it was part of a trilogy. This one, the Story of Jack Legs Diamond is part 1 of the trilogy. When I saw it on the shelf, I actually thought it was the one I'd read before. Not once I opened it. The era is the same, but the characters couldn't be more differnt. Any desire to read #2? No, not really, even if it was sitting on my shelf, I would not go directly there. Legs was an actual gangster who lived in died in the author's home town of Albany, New York. Its more or less a character study of Legs, and how he became a gangster of renown, and how he lost it all. Partially due to his own character flaws. Legs recognized his flaws, but seemed unwilling or unable to overcome them. This book was graphically violent, so if that's upsetting to you, skip this one. The narrator was, primarily, Leg's lawyer, Marcus. Marcus is attracted to everything about Leg's life, the money, the women, the violence. Marcus does not directly participate in any of the violence or crime -- but he does witness things, and does facilitate action. Marcus doesn't really feel bad about any of this. Should he? Marcus, early in the book compares himself to Nick in the Great Gatsby. Fitzgerald emphasized the glamourous wealthy side of crime; Kennedy emphasizes the actual violent and dirty crime side. They may dress beautifully, but dammit, someone's always dripping blood or vomit on the beautiful clothes. Both authors humanize their criminals. The period was rendered just as I imagine it to be (now, no idea if that's historically accurate or not). These gangsters are funny and witty. As well as hypocritical violent liars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5656736493685801647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3171971873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3171971873" />
    <a title="Mark Greenbaum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19596122-mark-greenbaum"><img alt="Mark Greenbaum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473035702p2/19596122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3171971873?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Greenbaum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Greenbaum" href="/user/show/19596122-mark-greenbaum">Mark Greenbaum</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3171971873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1591362371187683464">This is the first volume of Kennedy's Albany Cycle, a roving chronicle of the seedy and hardscrabble elements of Depression-era Albany, and it is a disappointment and the weakest entry of the opening trilogy. This one is about bootlegging mafioso Jack "Legs" Diamond, but the structuring here is strange and difficult to get a satisfying grasp on. Told principally through the eyes of his lawyer Marcus Gorman, it bounces back and forth between perspectives, creating confusion. The characters -- Dia</span>
  <span id="freeText1591362371187683464" style="display:none">This is the first volume of Kennedy's Albany Cycle, a roving chronicle of the seedy and hardscrabble elements of Depression-era Albany, and it is a disappointment and the weakest entry of the opening trilogy. This one is about bootlegging mafioso Jack "Legs" Diamond, but the structuring here is strange and difficult to get a satisfying grasp on. Told principally through the eyes of his lawyer Marcus Gorman, it bounces back and forth between perspectives, creating confusion. The characters -- Diamond's wife Alice, his moll Kiki, Gorman, his various hoodlums -- are, to a man and woman, wooden, unexplored, and so fully one-dimensional. The same applies to Diamond himself. For much of the book he is on the run, in a state of slow decline, but there really is no hard rise or hard fall; he exists in a strange stasis and ultimately Kennedy gives us absolutely no investment in him to give a damn in his fate. Nor is the writing as rich, whimsical, spiritual, or haunting as we see later in Ironweek, and before it, Billy Phelan. Kennedy was building to near-greatness but not there yet here. Totally skippable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1591362371187683464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8536447413614178682">Even though the cover blurb indicated that Legs Diamond was a real person, I didn't credit it until I looked him up after finishing the book.<br/><br/>Legs is such a consummate yarn. It even begins with a group of characters who have gathered in a bar to reminisce about the man, beginning with his storied death riddled with bullets on a hotel bed.   <span id="freeText8536447413614178682" style="display:none">Even though the cover blurb indicated that Legs Diamond was a real person, I didn't credit it until I looked him up after finishing the book.<br /><br />Legs is such a consummate yarn. It even begins with a group of characters who have gathered in a bar to reminisce about the man, beginning with his storied death riddled with bullets on a hotel bed. The stories are outlandish, the language is colorful. There are stolen gems, stills, speakeasies, easy women, gun battles. Events retailed have the outsized qualities of legend, and I read happily, willingly suspending my disbelief for the pleasure of the delivery.<br /><br />I am currently revisiting my impressions now that I know not only that Jack 'Legs' Diamond actually lived, but that the events described do not stray far from the facts. I think I prefer to believe that such awfulness lives only in the world of invention, even if our current administration proves this concept false every day.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12658830342864666228">American racketeering and icon-making link in Prohibition-era upstate New York. At one point Kennedy himself seems to fall victim to credulousness for his own celebrity character - and he almost lost me there, too, but he reins it in. The final scene, layered and pain-staking, I read again and again over the course of 4 days before I finally gave it up as something I understand right now as well as I can.<br/><br/>I suspect Kennedy is not read these days from a semi-false perception that he is a white mal</span>
  <span id="freeText12658830342864666228" style="display:none">American racketeering and icon-making link in Prohibition-era upstate New York. At one point Kennedy himself seems to fall victim to credulousness for his own celebrity character - and he almost lost me there, too, but he reins it in. The final scene, layered and pain-staking, I read again and again over the course of 4 days before I finally gave it up as something I understand right now as well as I can.<br /><br />I suspect Kennedy is not read these days from a semi-false perception that he is a white male writer who does white American romance, but there is a distinction readers don't make anymore that was a highly important one to his novels' settings and that is his subject - first generation Irish in the northeast, circa 1880 - 1930 - were not considered white in that era.<br /><br />I don't think his bibliography should be dismissed.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14509234987292694196">I had never heard of Jack "Legs" Diamond. I guess growing up in Chicago, we had a surplus of local 1920s Gangsters. And, embarrassingly, I did not realize Legs was an actual person until after starting the book. But, really, it didn't matter. The story is great -- fact or fiction. Like a lot of gangsters, Legs has his streaks of sadism and generosity that flip at schizopathic regularity. And I found myself torn alongside of our narrator, Legs' lawyer, who also finds Jack both appealing and appal</span>
  <span id="freeText14509234987292694196" style="display:none">I had never heard of Jack "Legs" Diamond. I guess growing up in Chicago, we had a surplus of local 1920s Gangsters. And, embarrassingly, I did not realize Legs was an actual person until after starting the book. But, really, it didn't matter. The story is great -- fact or fiction. Like a lot of gangsters, Legs has his streaks of sadism and generosity that flip at schizopathic regularity. And I found myself torn alongside of our narrator, Legs' lawyer, who also finds Jack both appealing and appalling. A great piece of historical fiction.</span>

This book sets forth excellent prose expositions of the protagonist and some of the characters surrounding him, including the narrator (his lawyer). This book, in assessing Depression-era America's fascination and (by a relatively vocal minority) admiration of "Legs," serves as a loose parable of some Am</span>

The narrator successfully portrays the "honest" self-actualization underlying the violence and venality of his client. This book, in assessing Depression-era America's fascination and (by a relatively vocal minority) admiration of "Legs," serves as a loose parable of some Americans' current idolatry of Donald Trump.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2brPO67JdxVpOecrbHLYzahtZ4H5ohA/J8FkW10aqmLS41aNCFP68Idz07+yXahZjTAqe24sko6128my9a6MHg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QzXrUm7mh9HKuUTtUpvPpXM+myZG8a9TEyd0NicHfzBIbHLkyHwKNCTzcHmMtL8xVmPW3NF/LeKBPdnfj7NZTA==')})
  }

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_7090'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7090.The_Soul_of_a_New_Machine?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Soul of a New Machine<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/4770.Tracy_Kidder?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Tracy Kidder<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.12 avg rating &mdash; 6,961 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1981\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2455115315119423179\">The computer revolution brought with it new methods of getting work done—just look at today\'s news for reports of hard-driven, highly-motivated young software and online commerce developers who sacrifice evenings and weekends to meet impossible deadl<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2455115315119423179\" style=\"display:none\">The computer revolution brought with it new methods of getting work done—just look at today\'s news for reports of hard-driven, highly-motivated young software and online commerce developers who sacrifice evenings and weekends to meet impossible deadlines. Tracy Kidder got a preview of this world in the late 1970s when he observed the engineers of Data General design and build a new 32-bit minicomputer in just one year. His thoughtful, prescient book, The Soul of a New Machine, tells stories of 35-year-old &quot;veteran&quot; engineers hiring recent college graduates and encouraging them to work harder and faster on complex and difficult projects, exploiting the youngsters\' ignorance of normal scheduling processes while engendering a new kind of work ethic.\n\nThese days, we are used to the &quot;total commitment&quot; philosophy of managing technical creation, but Kidder was surprised and even a little alarmed at the obsessions and compulsions he found. From in-house political struggles to workers being permitted to tease management to marathon 24-hour work sessions, The Soul of a New Machine explores concepts that already seem familiar, even old-hat, less than 20 years later. Kidder plainly admires his subjects; while he admits to hopeless confusion about their work, he finds their dedication heroic. The reader wonders, though, what will become of it all, now and in the future. —Rob Lightner<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2455115315119423179\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return //<![CDATA[

It tells the story of two young boxers out of Stockton, California: Ernie Munger and Billy Tully, one in his late teens, the other just turning<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1471353771567889678\" style=\"display:none\">Fat City is a novel about the indestructibility of of hope, the anguish and comedy of the human condition. It tells the story of two young boxers out of Stockton, California: Ernie Munger and Billy Tully, one in his late teens, the other just turning thirty, whose seemingly parallel lives intersect for a time. Set in an ambiance of glittering dreams and drab realities, it tells of the two fighters\' struggles to escape the confinements of their existence, and of the men and women in their world. Fat City is a novel about the sporting life like no other ever written: without melodrama or false heroics, written with a truthfulness that is at once painful and beautiful.\n\nDenis Johnson: &quot;Between the ages of 19 and 25 I studied Leonard Gardner’s book so closely that I began to fear I’d never be able to write anything but imitations of it, so I swore it off(...)When I was about 34 (the same age Gardner was when he published his), my first novel came out. About a year later I borrowed Fat City from the library and read it. I could see immediately that ten years’ exile hadn’t saved me from the influence of its perfection — I’d taught myself to write in Gardner’s style, though not as well. And now, many years later, it’s still true: Leonard Gardner has something to say in every word I write.&quot;\n\nJoan Didion: &quot;Leonard Gardner\'s Fat City affected me more than any new fiction I have read in a long while, and I do not think it affected me only because I come from Fat City, or somewhere near it. He has got it exactly right--the hanging around gas stations, the field dust, the relentless oppressiveness of the weather, the bleak liaisons sealed on the levees and Greyhound buses--but he has done more than just get it down, he has made it a metaphor for the joyless in heart.&quot;\n\nDavid Wagoner: &quot;The people he writes about are alive and three-dimensional, and have that meaty, sweaty immediacy I admire in novels and find so seldom. It\'s an odd, interesting world he explores here--as tense and vivid as the prose.&quot;\n\nIvan Gold: &quot;Gardner writes with power, with an insider\'s knowledge, and with a vividness and love for his characters which redeem them even when they\'re lost and beaten.&quot;\n\nHarry Mark Petrakis: &quot;A man of real talent. He makes the savage world he writes of come alive to the point where the reader can smell the sweat, and feel the anguish of unremitting failure.&quot;\n\nRoss Macdonald: &quot;In his pity and art Gardner moves beyond race, beyond guilt and punishment, as Twain and Melville did, into a tragic forgiveness. I have seldom read a novel as beautiful and individual as this one.&quot;\n\n\nOriginally published in 1969, Fat City is an American classic whose stature has increased over the years. Made into an acclaimed film by John Huston, the book is set in and around Stockton, California.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1471353771567889678\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10778973128531382126">William Joseph Kennedy is an American writer and journalist born and raised in Albany, New York. Many of his novels feature the interaction of members of the fictional Irish-American Phelan family, and make use of incidents of Albany's history and the supernatural. <br/><br/>Kennedy's works include <i>The Ink Truck</i> (1969), <i>Legs</i> (1975), <i>Billy Phelan's Greatest Game</i> (1978), <i>Ironweed</i> (1983, winner of 1984 Pulitze</span>
  <span id="freeText10778973128531382126" style="display:none">William Joseph Kennedy is an American writer and journalist born and raised in Albany, New York. Many of his novels feature the interaction of members of the fictional Irish-American Phelan family, and make use of incidents of Albany's history and the supernatural. <br /><br />Kennedy's works include <i>The Ink Truck</i> (1969), <i>Legs</i> (1975), <i>Billy Phelan's Greatest Game</i> (1978), <i>Ironweed</i> (1983, winner of 1984 Pulitzer Prize for Fiction; film, 1987), and <i>Roscoe</i> (2002).<br /><br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Joseph_Kennedy">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_...</a></span>
